				<div id="bodyContent" class="vector-body" aria-labelledby="firstHeading" data-mw-ve-target-container>
					<div class="vector-body-before-content">
							<div class="mw-indicators">
		</div>

						<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
					</div>
					<div id="contentSub"><div id="mw-content-subtitle"></div></div>
					
					
					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Language family of Japan</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1126788409">.mw-parser-output .plainlist ol,.mw-parser-output .plainlist ul{line-height:inherit;list-style:none;margin:0;padding:0}.mw-parser-output .plainlist ol li,.mw-parser-output .plainlist ul li{margin-bottom:0}</style><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output .infobox-subbox{padding:0;border:none;margin:-3px;width:auto;min-width:100%;font-size:100%;clear:none;float:none;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .infobox-3cols-child{margin:auto}.mw-parser-output .infobox .navbar{font-size:100%}body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-header,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-subheader,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-above,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-title,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-image,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-below{text-align:center}</style><table class="infobox" style="width:22em;"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" class="infobox-above" style="background-color: yellowgreen;">Japonic</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-subheader" style="background-color: yellowgreen;">Japanese–Ryukyuan or Japanic</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Geographic<br />distribution</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, possibly formerly on the <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korean Peninsula</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label"><a href="/wiki/Language_family" title="Language family">Linguistic classification</a></th><td class="infobox-data">One of the world's primary <a href="/wiki/Language_family" title="Language family">language families</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Proto-language</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Proto-Japonic" title="Proto-Japonic">Proto-Japonic</a></td></tr><tr class="plainlist"><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Subdivisions</th><td class="infobox-data">
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a></i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ryukyuan_languages" title="Ryukyuan languages">Ryukyuan</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hachij%C5%8D_language" title="Hachijō language">Hachijō</a></li>
<li><abbr title="Extinct" aria-label="Extinct" style="border: none; text-decoration: none; cursor: inherit; font-weight: normal; font-style: normal;">†</abbr><a href="/wiki/Peninsular_Japonic" title="Peninsular Japonic">Peninsular</a>?</li></ul>
</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label"><a href="/wiki/ISO_639-5" title="ISO 639-5">ISO 639-5</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><samp><a href="https://iso639-3.sil.org/code/jpx" class="extiw" title="iso639-3:jpx">jpx</a></samp></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label"><a href="/wiki/Glottolog" title="Glottolog">Glottolog</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><samp><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://glottolog.org/resource/languoid/id/japo1237">japo1237</a></samp></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-full-data"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Japanese_dialects-en.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Japanese_dialects-en.png/300px-Japanese_dialects-en.png" decoding="async" width="300" height="282" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Japanese_dialects-en.png/450px-Japanese_dialects-en.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Japanese_dialects-en.png/600px-Japanese_dialects-en.png 2x" data-file-width="2030" data-file-height="1905" /></a></span><div style="text-align:left;">Japonic languages and dialects</div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Japonic</b> or <b>Japanese–Ryukyuan</b> (<a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a>: <span lang="ja">日琉語族</span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Japanese" title="Romanization of Japanese">romanized</a>:&#160;</small><span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Nichiryū gozoku</i></span>), sometimes also <b>Japanic</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> is a <a href="/wiki/Language_family" title="Language family">language family</a> comprising <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a>, spoken in the main islands of Japan, and the <a href="/wiki/Ryukyuan_languages" title="Ryukyuan languages">Ryukyuan languages</a>, spoken in the <a href="/wiki/Ryukyu_Islands" title="Ryukyu Islands">Ryukyu Islands</a>. The family is universally accepted by <a href="/wiki/Linguist" class="mw-redirect" title="Linguist">linguists</a>, and significant progress has been made in reconstructing the <a href="/wiki/Proto-language" title="Proto-language">proto-language</a>, <a href="/wiki/Proto-Japonic" title="Proto-Japonic">Proto-Japonic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShimabukuro20071_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShimabukuro20071-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The reconstruction implies a split between all dialects of Japanese and all Ryukyuan varieties, probably before the 7th century. The <a href="/wiki/Hachij%C5%8D_language" title="Hachijō language">Hachijō language</a>, spoken on the <a href="/wiki/Izu_Islands" title="Izu Islands">Izu Islands</a>, is also included, but its position within the family is unclear.
</p><p>Most scholars believe that Japonic was brought to the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_archipelago" title="Japanese archipelago">Japanese archipelago</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Korean_peninsula" class="mw-redirect" title="Korean peninsula">Korean peninsula</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Yayoi_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Yayoi culture">Yayoi culture</a> during the 1st millennium BC. There is some fragmentary evidence suggesting that Japonic languages may still have been spoken in central and southern parts of the Korean peninsula (see <a href="/wiki/Peninsular_Japonic" title="Peninsular Japonic">Peninsular Japonic</a>) in the early centuries AD.
</p><p>Possible genetic relationships with many other language families have been proposed, most systematically with <a href="/wiki/Koreanic_languages" title="Koreanic languages">Koreanic</a>, but none have been conclusively demonstrated.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Japonic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Classification"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The extant Japonic languages belong to two well-defined branches: Japanese and Ryukyuan.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETranter20123_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETranter20123-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
Most scholars believe that Japonic was brought to northern Kyushu from the <a href="/wiki/Korean_peninsula" class="mw-redirect" title="Korean peninsula">Korean peninsula</a> around 700 to 300 BC by wet-rice farmers of the <a href="/wiki/Yayoi_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Yayoi culture">Yayoi culture</a> and spread throughout the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_archipelago" title="Japanese archipelago">Japanese archipelago</a>, replacing indigenous languages.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESerafim200898_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESerafim200898-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVovin2017_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVovin2017-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup>
The former wider distribution of <a href="/wiki/Ainu_languages" title="Ainu languages">Ainu languages</a> is confirmed by placenames in northern <a href="/wiki/Honshu" title="Honshu">Honshu</a> ending in <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">-betsu</i></span> (from Ainu <span title="Ainu (Japan)-language romanization"><i lang="ain-Latn">pet</i></span> 'river') and <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">-nai</i></span> (from Ainu <span title="Ainu (Japan)-language romanization"><i lang="ain-Latn">nai</i></span> 'stream').<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPatrie19824_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPatrie19824-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETamura2000269_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETamura2000269-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHudson199998_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHudson199998-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
Somewhat later, Japonic languages also spread southward to the <a href="/wiki/Ryukyu_Islands" title="Ryukyu Islands">Ryukyu Islands</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESerafim200898_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESerafim200898-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> There is fragmentary placename evidence that now-extinct Japonic languages were still spoken in central and southern parts of the Korean peninsula several centuries later.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVovin2013222–224_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVovin2013222–224-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESohn199935–36_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESohn199935–36-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Japanese">Japanese</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Japonic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Japanese"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese language</a></div>
<p>Japanese is the de facto national language of <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, where it is spoken by about 126 million people. The oldest attestation is <a href="/wiki/Old_Japanese" title="Old Japanese">Old Japanese</a>, which was recorded using <a href="/wiki/Chinese_characters" title="Chinese characters">Chinese characters</a> in the 7th and 8th centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrellesvig201012–20_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrellesvig201012–20-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
It differed from Modern Japanese in having a simple (C)V syllable structure and avoiding vowel sequences.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990121_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990121-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> The script also distinguished eight vowels (or diphthongs), with two each corresponding to modern <i>i</i>, <i>e</i> and <i>o</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990122_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990122-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Most of the texts reflect the speech of the area around <a href="/wiki/Nara,_Nara" class="mw-redirect" title="Nara, Nara">Nara</a>, the eighth-century Japanese capital, but over 300 poems were written in <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Old_Japanese" title="Eastern Old Japanese">eastern dialects of Old Japanese</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiyake2003159_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiyake2003159-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrellesvig201023–24,_151–153_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrellesvig201023–24,_151–153-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The language experienced a massive influx of <a href="/wiki/Sino-Japanese_vocabulary" title="Sino-Japanese vocabulary">Sino-Japanese vocabulary</a> after the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> in the 6th century and peaking with the wholesale importation of Chinese culture in the 8th and the 9th centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990120–121_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990120–121-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The loanwords now account for about half the lexicon.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990142–143_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990142–143-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> They also affected the sound system of the language by adding compound vowels, syllable-final nasals, and geminate consonants, which became separate <a href="/wiki/Mora_(linguistics)" title="Mora (linguistics)">morae</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990121–122,_167–170_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990121–122,_167–170-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
Most of the changes in morphology and syntax reflected in the modern language took place during the <a href="/wiki/Late_Middle_Japanese" title="Late Middle Japanese">Late Middle Japanese</a> period (13th to 16th centuries).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrellesvig20102,_326_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrellesvig20102,_326-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Modern mainland <a href="/wiki/Japanese_dialects" title="Japanese dialects">Japanese dialects</a>, spoken on <a href="/wiki/Honshu" title="Honshu">Honshu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kyushu" title="Kyushu">Kyushu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shikoku" title="Shikoku">Shikoku</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hokkaido" title="Hokkaido">Hokkaido</a>, are generally grouped as follows:<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990187,_189_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990187,_189-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Eastern Japanese, including most dialects from <a href="/wiki/Nagoya" title="Nagoya">Nagoya</a> east, including the modern standard <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_dialect" title="Tokyo dialect">Tokyo dialect</a>.</li>
<li>Western Japanese, including most dialects west of Nagoya, including the <a href="/wiki/Kansai_dialect#Kyoto" title="Kansai dialect">Kyoto dialect</a>.</li>
<li>Kyushu dialects, spoken on the island of Kyushu, including the <a href="/wiki/Kagoshima_dialect" title="Kagoshima dialect">Kagoshima dialect</a>/Satsugū dialect, spoken in <a href="/wiki/Kagoshima_Prefecture" title="Kagoshima Prefecture">Kagoshima Prefecture</a> in southern Kyushu.</li></ul>
<p>The early capitals of Nara and <a href="/wiki/Kyoto" title="Kyoto">Kyoto</a> lay within the western area, and their <a href="/wiki/Kansai_dialect" title="Kansai dialect">Kansai dialect</a> retained its prestige and influence long after the capital was moved to <a href="/wiki/Edo" title="Edo">Edo</a> (modern Tokyo) in 1603. Indeed, the Tokyo dialect has several western features not found in other eastern dialects.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShibatani19901999_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShibatani19901999-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Hachij%C5%8D_language" title="Hachijō language">Hachijō language</a>, spoken on <a href="/wiki/Hachij%C5%8D-jima" title="Hachijō-jima">Hachijō-jima</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Dait%C5%8D_Islands" title="Daitō Islands">Daitō Islands</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Aogashima,_Tokyo" title="Aogashima, Tokyo">Aogashima</a>, is highly divergent and varied. It has a mix of conservative features inherited from <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Old_Japanese" title="Eastern Old Japanese">Eastern Old Japanese</a> and influences from modern Japanese, making it difficult to classify.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990207_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990207-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPellard201516–17_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPellard201516–17-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPellard20182_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPellard20182-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Hachijō is an <a href="/wiki/Endangered_language" title="Endangered language">endangered language</a>, with a small population of elderly speakers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVovin2017_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVovin2017-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ryukyuan">Ryukyuan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Japonic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Ryukyuan"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ryukyuan_languages" title="Ryukyuan languages">Ryukyuan languages</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Ryukyu_Islands.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Ryukyu_Islands.svg/330px-Ryukyu_Islands.svg.png" decoding="async" width="330" height="228" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Ryukyu_Islands.svg/495px-Ryukyu_Islands.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Ryukyu_Islands.svg/660px-Ryukyu_Islands.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1186" data-file-height="818" /></a><figcaption>Southern and central Ryukyu islands</figcaption></figure>
<p>The Ryukyuan languages were originally and traditionally spoken throughout the <a href="/wiki/Ryukyu_Islands" title="Ryukyu Islands">Ryukyu Islands</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Island_arc" title="Island arc">island arc</a> stretching between the southern Japanese island of Kyushu and the <a href="/wiki/Island_of_Taiwan" class="mw-redirect" title="Island of Taiwan">island of Taiwan</a>. Most of them are considered "definitely" or "critically endangered" because of the spread of mainland Japanese.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShimoji2012352_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShimoji2012352-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Since Old Japanese displayed several innovations that are not shared with Ryukyuan, the two branches must have separated before the 7th century.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPellard201521–22_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPellard201521–22-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The move from Kyushu to the Ryukyus may have occurred later and possibly coincided with the rapid expansion of the agricultural <a href="/wiki/Gusuku_Sites_and_Related_Properties_of_the_Kingdom_of_Ryukyu" title="Gusuku Sites and Related Properties of the Kingdom of Ryukyu">Gusuku culture</a> in the 10th and 11th centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPellard201530–31_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPellard201530–31-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Such a date would explain the presence in Proto-Ryukyuan of Sino-Japanese vocabulary borrowed from <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Japanese" title="Early Middle Japanese">Early Middle Japanese</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPellard201523_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPellard201523-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> After the migration to the Ryukyus, there was limited influence from mainland Japan until the conquest of the <a href="/wiki/Ryukyu_Kingdom" title="Ryukyu Kingdom">Ryukyu Kingdom</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Satsuma_Domain" title="Satsuma Domain">Satsuma Domain</a> in 1609.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShimoji20104_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShimoji20104-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Ryukyuan varieties are considered dialects of Japanese in Japan but have little intelligibility with Japanese or even among one another.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990191_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990191-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> They are divided into northern and southern groups, corresponding to the physical division of the chain by the 250&#160;km-wide <a href="/wiki/Miyako_Strait" title="Miyako Strait">Miyako Strait</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShimoji2012352_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShimoji2012352-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Northern_Ryukyuan_languages" title="Northern Ryukyuan languages">Northern Ryukyuan languages</a> are spoken in the northern part of the chain, including the major <a href="/wiki/Amami_Islands" title="Amami Islands">Amami</a> and <a href="/wiki/Okinawa_Islands" title="Okinawa Islands">Okinawa Islands</a>. They form a single <a href="/wiki/Dialect_continuum" title="Dialect continuum">dialect continuum</a>, with mutual unintelligibility between widely separated varieties.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESerafim200880_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESerafim200880-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> The major varieties are, from northeast to southwest:<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrimes2003335_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrimes2003335-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Kikai_language" title="Kikai language">Kikai</a>, on the island of <a href="/wiki/Kikaijima" title="Kikaijima">Kikaijima</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Northern_Amami_%C5%8Cshima_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern Amami Ōshima language">Northern Amami Ōshima</a>, spoken in most of <a href="/wiki/Amami_%C5%8Cshima" title="Amami Ōshima">Amami Ōshima</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Southern_Amami_%C5%8Cshima_language" title="Southern Amami Ōshima language">Southern Amami Ōshima</a>, spoken in <a href="/wiki/Setouchi,_Kagoshima" title="Setouchi, Kagoshima">Setouchi</a> on the southern end of Amami Ōshima.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tokunoshima_language" title="Tokunoshima language">Tokunoshima</a>, on the island of <a href="/wiki/Tokunoshima" title="Tokunoshima">Tokunoshima</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Okinoerabu_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Okinoerabu language">Okinoerabu</a>, on the island of <a href="/wiki/Okinoerabujima" title="Okinoerabujima">Okinoerabujima</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Yoron_language" title="Yoron language">Yoron</a>, on the island of <a href="/wiki/Yoronjima" title="Yoronjima">Yoronjima</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kunigami_language" title="Kunigami language">Kunigami</a> or Northern Okinawan, spoken in the northern part of <a href="/wiki/Okinawa_Island" title="Okinawa Island">Okinawa Island</a>, including the cities of <a href="/wiki/Nakijin" class="mw-redirect" title="Nakijin">Nakijin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nago" title="Nago">Nago</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Okinawan_language" title="Okinawan language">(Central) Okinawan</a>, spoken in the central and southern parts of Okinawa Island, and neighboring islands. The prestige dialect is spoken in <a href="/wiki/Naha" title="Naha">Naha</a>, and the former city of <a href="/wiki/Shuri,_Okinawa" title="Shuri, Okinawa">Shuri</a>. The Shuri dialect was the lingua franca of the Ryukyuan Kingdom, and was first recorded in the 16th century, particularly in the <i><a href="/wiki/Omoro_S%C5%8Dshi" title="Omoro Sōshi">Omoro Sōshi</a></i> anthology.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShimoji2012352_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShimoji2012352-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETranter20124_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETranter20124-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>There is no agreement on the subgrouping of the varieties. One proposal, adopted by the UNESCO <i><a href="/wiki/Atlas_of_the_World%27s_Languages_in_Danger" title="Atlas of the World&#39;s Languages in Danger">Atlas of the World's Languages in Danger</a></i>, has three subgroups, with the central "Kunigami" branch comprising varieties from Southern Amami to Northern Okinawan, based on similar vowel systems and patterns of lenition of stops.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeinrichIshihara2017166_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeinrichIshihara2017166-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Pellard suggests a binary division based on shared innovations, with an Amami group including the varieties from Kikai to Yoron, and an Okinawa group comprising the varieties of Okinawa and smaller islands to its west.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPellard201517–18_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPellard201517–18-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Southern_Ryukyuan_languages" title="Southern Ryukyuan languages">Southern Ryukyuan languages</a> are spoken in the southern part of the chain, the <a href="/wiki/Sakishima_Islands" title="Sakishima Islands">Sakishima Islands</a>. They comprise three distinct dialect continua:<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESerafim200880_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESerafim200880-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Miyakoan_language" title="Miyakoan language">Miyako</a> is spoken in the <a href="/wiki/Miyako_Islands" title="Miyako Islands">Miyako Islands</a>, with dialects on <a href="/wiki/Irabu_Island" title="Irabu Island">Irabu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tarama,_Okinawa" title="Tarama, Okinawa">Tarama</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Yaeyama_language" title="Yaeyama language">Yaeyama</a> is spoken in the <a href="/wiki/Yaeyama_Islands" title="Yaeyama Islands">Yaeyama Islands</a> (except Yonaguni), with dialects on each island, but primarily <a href="/wiki/Ishigaki_Island" title="Ishigaki Island">Ishigaki Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iriomote_Island" title="Iriomote Island">Iriomote Island</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Taketomi_Island" title="Taketomi Island">Taketomi Island</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Yonaguni_language" title="Yonaguni language">Yonaguni</a>, spoken on <a href="/wiki/Yonaguni_Island" class="mw-redirect" title="Yonaguni Island">Yonaguni Island</a>, is phonologically distinct but lexically closer to other Yaeyama varieties.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990194_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990194-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>The southern Ryukyus were settled by Japonic-speakers from the northern Ryukyus in the 13th century, leaving no linguistic trace of the indigenous inhabitants of the islands.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShimoji20104_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShimoji20104-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternative_classifications">Alternative classifications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Japonic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Alternative classifications"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>An alternative classification, based mainly on the development of the <a href="/wiki/Pitch_accent" class="mw-redirect" title="Pitch accent">pitch accent</a>, groups the highly divergent <a href="/wiki/Kagoshima_dialect" title="Kagoshima dialect">Kagoshima dialects</a> of southwestern <a href="/wiki/Kyushu" title="Kyushu">Kyushu</a> with Ryukyuan in a Southwestern branch.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShimabukuro20072,_41–43_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShimabukuro20072,_41–43-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
Kyushu and Ryukyuan varieties also share some lexical items, some of which appear to be innovations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEde_Boer202055_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Boer202055-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
The internal classification by Elisabeth de Boer includes Ryukyuan as a deep subbranch of a Kyūshū–Ryūkyū branch:<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEde_Boer202052_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Boer202052-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1116488514">.mw-parser-output .treeview ul{padding:0;margin:0}.mw-parser-output .treeview li{padding:0;margin:0;list-style-type:none;list-style-image:none}.mw-parser-output .treeview li li{background:url("https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f2/Treeview-grey-line.png")no-repeat 0 -2981px;padding-left:21px;text-indent:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .treeview li li:last-child{background-position:0 -5971px}.mw-parser-output .treeview li.emptyline>ul>.mw-empty-elt:first-child+.emptyline,.mw-parser-output .treeview li.emptyline>ul>li:first-child{background-position:0 9px}</style><div class="treeview">
<ul><li><b>Japonic</b>
<ul><li>Eastern</li>
<li>Central</li>
<li>Izumo–Tōhoku</li>
<li>Kyūshū–Ryūkyū</li></ul></li></ul>
</div>
<p>She also proposes a branch consisting of the <a href="/wiki/Umpaku_dialect" title="Umpaku dialect">Izumo dialect</a> (spoken on the northern coast of western Honshu) and the <a href="/wiki/T%C5%8Dhoku_dialect" title="Tōhoku dialect">Tōhoku dialects</a> (northern Honshu), which show similar developments in the pitch accent that she attributes to sea-borne contacts.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEde_Boer202058_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Boer202058-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Another alternative classification, proposed by the <a href="/wiki/Max_Planck_Institute_for_Evolutionary_Anthropology" title="Max Planck Institute for Evolutionary Anthropology">Max Planck Institute for Evolutionary Anthropology</a> as part of their <a href="/wiki/Glottolog" title="Glottolog">Glottolog</a> project, splits the Hachijō language into an independent branch of Japonic, in addition to splitting the divergent Kagoshima and <a href="/wiki/Tsugaru_dialect" title="Tsugaru dialect">Tsugaru</a> dialects into independent branches of a "Japanesic" family.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1116488514"><div class="treeview">
<ul><li><b>Japonic</b>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Hachij%C5%8D_language" title="Hachijō language">Hachijo</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Japanese_dialects" title="Japanese dialects">Japanesic</a>
<ul><li>Japan−Taiwan Japanese
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Yilan_Creole_Japanese" title="Yilan Creole Japanese">Yilan Creole Japanese</a></li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kagoshima_dialect" title="Kagoshima dialect">Kagoshima</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Old_Japanese" title="Old Japanese">Old Japanese</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tsugaru_dialect" title="Tsugaru dialect">Tsugaru</a></li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ryukyuan_languages" title="Ryukyuan languages">Ryukyuan</a></li></ul></li></ul>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Peninsular_Japonic">Peninsular Japonic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Japonic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Peninsular Japonic"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Peninsular_Japonic" title="Peninsular Japonic">Peninsular Japonic</a></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Placename_glosses_in_the_Samguk_sagi" title="Placename glosses in the Samguk sagi">Placename glosses in the Samguk sagi</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:History_of_Korea-476.PNG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/History_of_Korea-476.PNG/170px-History_of_Korea-476.PNG" decoding="async" width="170" height="201" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/History_of_Korea-476.PNG/255px-History_of_Korea-476.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/History_of_Korea-476.PNG/340px-History_of_Korea-476.PNG 2x" data-file-width="970" data-file-height="1146" /></a><figcaption>Korea in the late 5th century</figcaption></figure>
<p>There is fragmentary evidence suggesting that now-extinct Japonic languages were spoken in the central and southern parts of the Korean peninsula.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVovin2013222–224_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVovin2013222–224-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESohn199935–36_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESohn199935–36-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Vovin calls these languages Peninsular Japonic and groups Japanese and Ryukyuan as <a href="/w/index.php?title=Insular_Japonic&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Insular Japonic (page does not exist)">Insular Japonic</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Langues_japoniques_insulaires" class="extiw" title="fr:Langues japoniques insulaires">fr</a>&#93;</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVovin2017_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVovin2017-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The most-cited evidence comes from chapter 37 of the <span title="Korean-language romanization"><i lang="ko-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Samguk_sagi" title="Samguk sagi">Samguk sagi</a></i></span> (compiled in 1145), which contains a list of pronunciations and meanings of placenames in the former kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Goguryeo" title="Goguryeo">Goguryeo</a>. As the pronunciations are given using <a href="/wiki/Chinese_characters" title="Chinese characters">Chinese characters</a>, they are difficult to interpret, but several of those from central Korea, in the area south of the <a href="/wiki/Han_River_(Korea)" title="Han River (Korea)">Han River</a> captured from <a href="/wiki/Baekje" title="Baekje">Baekje</a> in the 5th century, seem to correspond to Japonic words.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVovin2017_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVovin2017-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeeRamsey201137–43_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeeRamsey201137–43-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Scholars differ on whether they represent the language of Goguryeo or the people that it conquered.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVovin2017_5-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVovin2017-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckwith200750–92_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckwith200750–92-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Traces from the south of the peninsula are very sparse:
</p>
<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Silla" title="Silla">Silla</a> placenames listed in Chapter 34 of the <span title="Korean-language romanization"><i lang="ko-Latn">Samguk sagi</i></span> are not glossed, but many of them can be explained as Japonic words.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVovin2017_5-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVovin2017-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Alexander_Vovin" title="Alexander Vovin">Alexander Vovin</a> proposes Japonic etymologies for two of four Baekje words given in the <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Liang" title="Book of Liang">Book of Liang</a></i> (635).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVovin2013232_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVovin2013232-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>A single word is explicitly attributed to the language of the southern <a href="/wiki/Gaya_confederacy" title="Gaya confederacy">Gaya confederacy</a>, in Chapter 44 of the <span title="Korean-language romanization"><i lang="ko-Latn">Samguk sagi</i></span>. It is a word for 'gate' and appears in a similar form to the <a href="/wiki/Old_Japanese" title="Old Japanese">Old Japanese</a> word <span title="Old Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ojp-Latn">to<sub>2</sub></i></span>, with the same meaning.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeeRamsey201146–47_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeeRamsey201146–47-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckwith200740_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckwith200740-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Vovin suggests that the ancient name for the kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Tamna" title="Tamna">Tamna</a> on <a href="/wiki/Jeju_Island" title="Jeju Island">Jeju Island</a>, <span title="undetermined-language text"><i lang="und">tammura</i></span>, may have a Japonic etymology <span title="Old Japanese-language text"><i lang="ojp">tani mura</i></span> 'valley settlement' or <span title="Old Japanese-language text"><i lang="ojp">tami mura</i></span> 'people's settlement'.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVovin2013236–237_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVovin2013236–237-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Proposed_external_relationships">Proposed external relationships</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Japonic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Proposed external relationships"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Classification_of_the_Japonic_languages" title="Classification of the Japonic languages">Classification of the Japonic languages</a></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Comparison_of_Japanese_and_Korean" title="Comparison of Japanese and Korean">Comparison of Japanese and Korean</a></div>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Shir%C5%8D_Hattori" title="Shirō Hattori">Shirō Hattori</a>, more attempts have been made to link Japanese with other language families than for any other language.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKindaichi197831_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKindaichi197831-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> None of the attempts has succeeded in demonstrating a common descent for Japonic and any other language family.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVovin2017_5-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVovin2017-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The most systematic comparisons have involved <a href="/wiki/Korean_language" title="Korean language">Korean</a>, which has a very similar grammatical structure to Japonic languages.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVovin20103_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVovin20103-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Elmo_Martin" class="mw-redirect" title="Samuel Elmo Martin">Samuel Elmo Martin</a>, John Whitman, and others have proposed hundreds of possible cognates, with sound correspondences.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVovin2017_5-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVovin2017-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShibatani199099–100_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShibatani199099–100-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESohn199929–35_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESohn199929–35-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> However, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Vovin" title="Alexander Vovin">Alexander Vovin</a> points out that Old Japanese contains several pairs of words of similar meaning in which one word matches a Korean form, and the other is also found in Ryukyuan and Eastern Old Japanese, suggesting that the former is an early loan from Korean.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVovin201092–94_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVovin201092–94-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> He suggests that to eliminate such early loans, Old Japanese morphemes should not be assigned a Japonic origin unless they are also attested in Southern Ryukyuan or Eastern Old Japanese.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVovin20106_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVovin20106-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> That procedure leaves fewer than a dozen possible cognates, which may have been borrowed by Korean from Peninsular Japonic.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVovin2010237–240_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVovin2010237–240-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Typology">Typology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Japonic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Typology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Most Japonic languages have <a href="/wiki/Voice_(phonetics)" title="Voice (phonetics)">voicing</a> opposition for <a href="/wiki/Obstruent" title="Obstruent">obstruents</a>, with exceptions such as the Miyako dialect of Ōgami.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShimoji20104–5_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShimoji20104–5-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Glottalized" class="mw-redirect" title="Glottalized">Glottalized</a> consonants are common in North Ryukyuan languages but are rarer in South Ryukyuan.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShimoji20105_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShimoji20105-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990194_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990194-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
Proto-Japonic had only voiceless obstruents, like <a href="/wiki/Ainu_languages" title="Ainu languages">Ainu</a> and proto-<a href="/wiki/Korean_language" title="Korean language">Korean</a>.
Japonic languages also resemble Ainu and modern Korean in having a single <a href="/wiki/Liquid_consonant" title="Liquid consonant">liquid consonant</a> phoneme.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETranter20127_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETranter20127-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
A five-vowel system like Standard Japanese <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/a/</span>, <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/i/</span>, <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/u/</span>, <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/e/</span> and <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/o/</span> is common, but some Ryukyuan languages also have central vowels <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ə/</span> and <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɨ/</span>, and Yonaguni has only <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/a/</span>, <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/i/</span>, and <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/u/</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShimoji2012352_28-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShimoji2012352-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIzuyama2012413_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIzuyama2012413-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In most Japonic languages, <a href="/wiki/Isochrony" title="Isochrony">speech rhythm</a> is based on a subsyllabic unit, the <a href="/wiki/Mora_(linguistics)" title="Mora (linguistics)">mora</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShimoji20106_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShimoji20106-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Each syllable has a basic mora of the form (C)V but a <a href="/wiki/Japanese_phonology#Moraic_nasal" title="Japanese phonology">nasal coda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gemination" title="Gemination">geminate consonant</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Vowel_length" title="Vowel length">lengthened vowel</a> counts as an additional mora.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990158–159_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990158–159-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> However, some dialects in northern Honshu or southern Kyushu have syllable-based rhythm.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990160_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990160-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Like Ainu, <a href="/wiki/Middle_Korean" title="Middle Korean">Middle Korean</a>, and some modern <a href="/wiki/Korean_dialects" title="Korean dialects">Korean dialects</a>, most Japonic varieties have a lexical <a href="/wiki/Pitch_accent" class="mw-redirect" title="Pitch accent">pitch accent</a>, which governs whether the moras of a word are pronounced high or low, but it follows widely-different patterns.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETranter20127_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETranter20127-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShimoji20107_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShimoji20107-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> In Tokyo-type systems, the basic pitch of a word is high, with an accent (if present) marking the position of a drop to low pitch.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990180–181_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990180–181-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> In Kyushu dialects, the basic pitch is low, with accented syllables given high pitch.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990182_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990182-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> In Kyoto-type systems, both types are used.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990182–184_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990182–184-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Japonic languages, again like Ainu and Korean, are <a href="/wiki/Left-branching" class="mw-redirect" title="Left-branching">left-branching</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Head-final" class="mw-redirect" title="Head-final">head-final</a>), with a basic <a href="/wiki/Subject%E2%80%93object%E2%80%93verb" class="mw-redirect" title="Subject–object–verb">subject–object–verb</a> word order, modifiers before nouns, and <a href="/wiki/Postposition" class="mw-redirect" title="Postposition">postpositions</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETranter20126_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETranter20126-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShimoji20108_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShimoji20108-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> There is a clear distinction between verbs, which have extensive <a href="/wiki/Inflection" title="Inflection">inflectional</a> morphology, and nominals, with <a href="/wiki/Agglutinative" class="mw-redirect" title="Agglutinative">agglutinative</a> suffixing morphology.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShimoji20109–10_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShimoji20109–10-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShimoji202211_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShimoji202211-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
Mainland varieties have adjectives of both types, while Ryukyuan languages inflect all adjectives in the same way as verbs.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShimoji202214–15_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShimoji202214–15-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Most Japonic languages mark singular and plural <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_number" title="Grammatical number">number</a>, but some Northern Ryukyuan languages also have the <a href="/wiki/Dual_(grammatical_number)" title="Dual (grammatical number)">dual</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShimoji202211_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShimoji202211-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
Most Ryukyuan languages mark a <a href="/wiki/Clusivity" title="Clusivity">clusivity</a> distinction in plural (or dual) first-person pronouns, but no Mainland varieties do so.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShimoji202213_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShimoji202213-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
The most common type of <a href="/wiki/Morphosyntactic_alignment" title="Morphosyntactic alignment">morphosyntactic alignment</a> is <a href="/wiki/Nominative%E2%80%93accusative_alignment" title="Nominative–accusative alignment">nominative–accusative</a>, but neutral (or direct), <a href="/wiki/Active%E2%80%93stative_alignment" title="Active–stative alignment">active–stative</a> and (very rarely) <a href="/wiki/Tripartite_alignment" title="Tripartite alignment">tripartite alignment</a> are found in some Japonic languages.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShimoji202215–18_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShimoji202215–18-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Proto-Japonic">Proto-Japonic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Japonic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Proto-Japonic"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Proto-Japonic" title="Proto-Japonic">Proto-Japonic</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Proto-language" title="Proto-language">proto-language</a> of the family has been reconstructed by using a combination of <a href="/wiki/Internal_reconstruction" title="Internal reconstruction">internal reconstruction</a> from Old Japanese and by applying the <a href="/wiki/Comparative_method" title="Comparative method">comparative method</a> to Old Japanese (including eastern dialects) and Ryukyuan.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrellesvigWhitman20081_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrellesvigWhitman20081-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> The major reconstructions of the 20th century were produced by <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Elmo_Martin" class="mw-redirect" title="Samuel Elmo Martin">Samuel Elmo Martin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shir%C5%8D_Hattori" title="Shirō Hattori">Shirō Hattori</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrellesvigWhitman20081_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrellesvigWhitman20081-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMartin1987_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMartin1987-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Proto-Japonic words are generally polysyllabic, with syllables having the form (C)V. The following proto-Japonic consonant inventory is generally agreed upon, except that some scholars argue for voiced stops <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">*b</span> and <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">*d</span> instead of glides <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">*w</span> and <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">*j</span>:<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrellesvigWhitman20083_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrellesvigWhitman20083-78">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<p>The Old Japanese voiced consonants <i>b</i>, <i>d</i>, <i>z</i> and <i>g</i>, which never occurred word-initially, are derived from clusters of nasals and voiceless consonants after the loss of an intervening vowel.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhitman201227_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhitman201227-79">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Most authors accept six Proto-Japonic vowels:<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhitman201226_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhitman201226-80">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center">
<caption>Proto-Japonic vowels
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th>
</th>
<th><a href="/wiki/Front_vowel" title="Front vowel">Front</a>
</th>
<th><a href="/wiki/Central_vowel" title="Central vowel">Central</a>
</th>
<th><a href="/wiki/Back_vowel" title="Back vowel">Back</a>
</th></tr>
<tr>
<th><a href="/wiki/Close_vowel" title="Close vowel">Close</a>
</th>
<td><span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">*i</span>
</td>
<td>
</td>
<td><span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">*u</span>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th><a href="/wiki/Mid_vowel" title="Mid vowel">Mid</a>
</th>
<td><span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">*e</span>
</td>
<td><span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">*ə</span>
</td>
<td><span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">*o</span>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th><a href="/wiki/Open_vowel" title="Open vowel">Open</a>
</th>
<td>
</td>
<td><span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">*a</span>
</td>
<td>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Some authors also propose a high central vowel <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">*ɨ</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrellesvig201045–47_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrellesvig201045–47-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVovin201035–36_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVovin201035–36-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
The mid vowels <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">*e</span> and <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">*o</span> were raised to <i>i</i> and <i>u</i> respectively in Old Japanese, except word-finally.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrellesvigWhitman20085_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrellesvigWhitman20085-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrellesvig201047_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrellesvig201047-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
Other Old Japanese vowels arose from sequences of Proto-Japonic vowels.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrellesvig201050_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrellesvig201050-85">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It is generally accepted that a lexical <a href="/wiki/Pitch_accent" class="mw-redirect" title="Pitch accent">pitch accent</a> should be reconstructed for Proto-Japonic, but its precise form is controversial.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhitman201227_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhitman201227-79">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Japonic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Notes"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Roy_Andrew_Miller" title="Roy Andrew Miller">Roy Andrew Miller</a> identified the arrival of Japonic with the <a href="/wiki/Early_J%C5%8Dmon" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Jōmon">Early Jōmon</a> period (<span title="circa">c.</span><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;3000 BC</span>), but this is difficult to reconcile with the relatively shallow depth of Japonic and the presence of Japonic placenames on the Korean peninsula in the 1st millennium AD.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHudson199986–87_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHudson199986–87-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhitman2011155_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhitman2011155-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
